Get equipped to hunt\",\"image\":\"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/03_ThreatIntelligenceWebsiteBannerIdeas_BA.max-2600x2600.png\",\"author\":[{\"@type\":\"Person\",\"name\":\"Mandiant \",\"url\":\"\"}],\"datePublished\":\"2021-12-10\",\"publisher\":{\"@type\":\"Organization\",\"name\":\"Google Cloud\",\"logo\":{\"@type\":\"ImageObject\",\"url\":\"https://www.gstatic.com/devrel-devsite/prod/v8bb8fa0afe9a8c3a776ebeb25d421bb443344d789b3607754dfabea418b8c4be/cloud/images/cloud-logo.svg\"}},\"url\":\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/defining-cobalt-strike-components\",\"keywords\":[\"Threat Intelligence\"],\"timeRequired\":\"PT21M\"}\u003c/script\u003e"],["Defining Cobalt Strike Components So You Can BEA-CONfident in Your Analysis"],null,null,[[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eWritten by: Alyssa Rahman\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003chr\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eCobalt Strike\u003c/strong\u003e is a commercial adversary simulation software that is marketed to red teams but is also stolen and actively used by a wide range of threat actors from ransomware operators to espionage-focused Advanced Persistent Threats (APTs). Many network defenders have seen Cobalt Strike payloads used in intrusions, but for those who have not had the opportunity to use Cobalt Strike as an operator, it can be challenging to understand the many components and features included in this framework.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn this blog post, we will walk through important definitions and concepts to help defenders understand Cobalt Strike and, hopefully, identify new ways to hunt for, respond to, and attribute malicious actors using this tool.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis content is informed by a combination of official documentation, public research, and Mandiant’s own experience with both performing red team assessments and responding to intrusions where threat actors are using Cobalt Strike. Because Cobalt Strike is used for both legitimate testing and for intrusions, in this post we will refer to all Cobalt Strike users, regardless of motivation, as \"operators\" for simplicity.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhile this blog post is focused on explaining Cobalt Strike and BEACON artifacts to aid defenders’ analysis, we have also included references to several resources to learn more about Cobalt Strike and how to detect common BEACON activity at various stages of the attack lifecycle.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eImportant Components\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eCobalt Strike, BEACON, Team Server. Oh My!\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eYou may hear the names Cobalt Strike, BEACON, and even team server used interchangeably, but there are some important distinctions between all of them.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eCobalt Strike\u003c/strong\u003e is the \u003cem\u003ecommand and control \u003c/em\u003e(\u003cem\u003eC2) application\u003c/em\u003e itself. This has two primary components: the team server and the client. These are both contained in the same Java executable (JAR file) and the only difference is what arguments an operator uses to execute it.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eTeam server \u003c/strong\u003eis the C2 server portion of Cobalt Strike. It can accept client connections, BEACON callbacks, and general web requests.\r\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eBy default, it accepts client connections on TCP port 50050.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eTeam server only supports being run on Linux systems.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eClient\u003c/strong\u003e is how operators connect to a team server.\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eClients can run on the same system as a Team server or connect remotely.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eClient can be run on Windows, macOS or Linux systems.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBEACON\u003c/strong\u003e is the name for Cobalt Strike’s default malware payload used to create a connection to the team server. Active callback sessions from a target are also called \"beacons\". (This is where the malware family got its name.) There are two types of BEACON:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThe \u003cstrong\u003eStager\u003c/strong\u003e is an optional BEACON payload. Operators can \"stage\" their malware by sending an initial small BEACON shellcode payload that only does some basic checks and then queries the configured C2 for the fully featured backdoor.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThe \u003cstrong\u003eFull\u003c/strong\u003e \u003cstrong\u003ebackdoor \u003c/strong\u003ecan either be executed through a BEACON stager, by a “loader” malware family, or by directly executing the default DLL export “ReflectiveLoader”. This backdoor runs in memory and can establish a connection to the team server through several methods.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eLoaders\u003c/strong\u003e are \u003cem\u003enot\u003c/em\u003e BEACON. BEACON is the backdoor itself and is typically executed with some other loader, whether it is the staged or full backdoor. Cobalt Strike does come with default loaders, but operators can also create their own using PowerShell, .NET, C++, GoLang, or really anything capable of running shellcode.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eIt’s All Connected\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.cobaltstrike.com/help-listener-management\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eListeners\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e are the Cobalt Strike component that payloads, such as BEACON, use to connect to a team server. Cobalt Strike supports several protocols and supports a wide range of modifications within each listener type. Some changes to a listener require a \"listener restart\" and generating a new payload. Some changes require a full team server restart.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.cobaltstrike.com/help-http-beacon\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eHTTP/HTTPS\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e is by far the most common listener type.\r\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eWhile Cobalt Strike includes a default TLS certificate, this is well known to defenders and blocked by many enterprise products (“signatured”). Usually operators will generate valid certificates, such as with LetsEncrypt, for their C2 domains to blend in.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThanks to Malleable Profiles (discussed later in the post), operators can heavily configure how the BEACON network traffic will look and can masquerade as legitimate HTTP connections.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eOperators can provide a list of domains/IPs when configuring a listener, and the team server will accept BEACON connections from all of them (see \"Redirectors\"). Operators can also specify Host header values (see \"Domain Fronting\").\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.cobaltstrike.com/help-dns-beacon\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDNS\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e listeners establish sessions to their team server using DNS requests for domains the team server is authoritative for. DNS listeners support \u003ca href\u003d\"https://blog.cobaltstrike.com/2013/06/20/thatll-never-work-we-dont-allow-port-53-out/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003etwo modes\u003c/a\u003e:\r\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eHybrid (DNS+HTTP)\u003c/strong\u003e is the default and uses DNS for a beacon channel and HTTP for a data channel.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePure DNS\u003c/strong\u003e can also be enabled to use DNS for both beacon and data channels. This leverages regular A record requests to avoid using HTTPS and provide a stealthier, though slower method of communication.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.cobaltstrike.com/help-smb-beacon\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eSMB \u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003eis a bind style listener and is most often used for chaining beacons. Bind listeners open a local port on a targeted system and wait for an incoming connection from an operator. See \u0026quot;Important Concepts \u0026gt; Chaining Beacons\u0026quot; for more information.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.cobaltstrike.com/help-tcp-beacon\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eRaw TCP\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e is a (newer) bind style listener and can also be used for chaining beacons. See \u0026quot;Important Concepts \u0026gt; Chaining Beacons\u0026quot; for more information.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe final two listeners are less common, but they provide compatibility with other payload types.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.cobaltstrike.com/help-listener-management\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eForeign listeners\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e allow connections from Metasploit’s \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.offensive-security.com/metasploit-unleashed/about-meterpreter/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eMeterpreter\u003c/a\u003e backdoor to simplify passing sessions between the Metasploit framework and the Cobalt Strike framework.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.cobaltstrike.com/help-externalc2\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eExternal C2\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e listeners provide a specification that operators can use to connect to a team server with a reverse TCP listener. Reverse listeners connect back and establish an external connection to an operator, instead of waiting for an incoming connection such as with “bind” listeners.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eIt’s (Almost) All Customizable\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://cobaltstrike.com/scripts\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eArsenal Kits\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e \u003c/strong\u003eare available for download, with a valid license, and for use with licensed (or cracked) installations only. Arsenal kits are sometimes distributed with cracked copies of Cobalt Strike. The full list of kits (as of October 2021) is:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://blog.cobaltstrike.com/2017/10/03/kits-profiles-and-scripts-oh-my/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eApplet/PowerApplet Kit\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e allows operators to modify Cobalt Strike’s built-in Java Applet payloads. This kit was the first to be added to Arsenal and is no longer widely used.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.cobaltstrike.com/help-artifact-kit\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eArtifact Kit\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e allows operators to modify the templates for all Cobalt Strike executables, DLLs, and shellcode. This kit was added in January 2014 and is still used.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.cobaltstrike.com/help-elevate\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eElevate Kit\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e allows operators to integrate their privilege escalation exploits as Cobalt Strike commands. This kit was released in December 2016. Unlike other kits, this is \u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/rsmudge/ElevateKit\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003epublic\u003c/a\u003e, and it is essentially just an Aggressor Script to streamline loading your own PowerShell scripts, binaries, etc. into a BEACON session (see “Aggressor Scripts”).\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://blog.cobaltstrike.com/2021/07/29/introducing-mimikatz-kit/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMimikatz Kit\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e allows operators to update their version of Mimikatz without waiting for a Cobalt Strike software update. This kit was added in July 2021.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.cobaltstrike.com/help-resource-kit\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eResource Kit\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e allows operators to modify the script templates Cobalt Strike uses (mostly as loaders). This kit was added in May 2017 and is still used.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.cobaltstrike.com/help-sleep-mask-kit\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eSleep Mask Kit\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e allows operators to modify the in-memory obfuscation that BEACON uses to avoid detection methods such as \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/detecting-cobalt-strike-with-memory-signatures\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003ethese\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e. This kit was added in August 2021. For a walkthrough on how this kit can be used to affect in-memory BEACON detection check out \u003ca href\u003d\"https://adamsvoboda.net/sleeping-with-a-mask-on-cobaltstrike/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003ethis blog post\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.cobaltstrike.com/help-user-defined-reflective-loader\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eUser Defined Reflective Loaders Kit\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e allows operators to customize the reflective loader functionality used by BEACON. This kit was added in August 2021.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.cobaltstrike.com/help-malleable-c2\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMalleable Profile \u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003eis the final part of Arsenal Kit, and it allows operators to extensively modify how their Cobalt Strike installation works. It is the most common way operators customize Cobalt Strike and has thus been \u003ca href\u003d\"https://blog.zsec.uk/cobalt-strike-profiles/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eheavily\u003c/a\u003e \u003ca href\u003d\"https://posts.specterops.io/a-deep-dive-into-cobalt-strike-malleable-c2-6660e33b0e0b\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003edocumented\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eChanges to a Malleable Profile require a team server restart and, depending on the change, may require re-generating payloads and re-spawning beacon sessions.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThere are several robust open-source projects that generate randomized profiles which can make detection challenging. Still, operators will often reuse profiles (or only slightly modify them) allowing for easier detection and potentially attribution clustering.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eWhen analyzing samples, check GitHub and other public sources to see if the profile is open source.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.cobaltstrike.com/help-scripting\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAggressor Scripts \u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003eare macros that operators can write and load in their client to streamline their workflow. These are loaded and executed within the client context and don't create new BEACON functionality, so much as automate existing commands. They are written in a Perl-based language called \"Sleep\" which Raphael Mudge (the creator of Cobalt Strike) wrote. For an example, check out the \"An Operator's View\" section.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAggressor scripts are \u003cem\u003eonly\u003c/em\u003e loaded into an operator's local Client. They are \u003cem\u003enot\u003c/em\u003e loaded into other operators’ clients, the team server, or BEACON sessions (victim hosts). The \u003cu\u003eprimary detection opportunity\u003c/u\u003e for these scripts is reviewing them for static defaults that can be used in detection or hunting rules.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://blog.cobaltstrike.com/2018/04/09/cobalt-strike-3-11-the-snake-that-eats-its-tail/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eExecute-Assembly\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e \u003c/strong\u003eis a BEACON command that allows operators to run a .NET executable in memory on a targeted host. BEACON runs these executables by spawning a temporary process and injecting the assembly into it. In contrast to Aggressor Scripts, execute-assembly does allow operators to extend BEACON functionality. Assemblies run in this way will still be scanned by Microsoft’s \u003ca href\u003d\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/amsi/antimalware-scan-interface-portal\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eAMSI\u003c/a\u003e if it is enabled.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.cobaltstrike.com/help-beacon-object-files\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBeacon Object Files\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e (BOFs)\u003c/strong\u003e are a fairly recent Cobalt Strike feature that allows operators to extend BEACON post-exploitation functionality. BOFs are compiled C programs that are executed in memory on a targeted host. In contrast to Aggressor Scripts, BOFs are loaded within a BEACON session and can create new BEACON capabilities. Additionally, compared to other BEACON post-exploitation commands like execute-assembly, BOFs are relatively stealthy as they run within a BEACON session and do not require a process creation or injection.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eAn Operator’s View\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFour of the aforementioned components—the Client, Aggressor Scripts, Beacon Object Files, and Malleable Profiles—are the primary ways operators interact with and customize their team server. An example of each is included here to show these components from an operator’s perspective.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAn operator accessing a team server through the Cobalt Strike \u003cstrong\u003eclient \u003c/strong\u003ewould see a view like the following. The top pane shows a list of active beacon sessions with basic metadata including the current user, process ID, internal and external IP addresses, and the last time the host checked in with the team server. The bottom pane includes a tab for each session where operators can send commands to the victim hosts and see a log of past commands and output. The client interface also allows operators to build payloads, execute plugins, and generate reports.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 1: Cobalt Strike 3.10 client view (\u003ca href\u003d\"https://hstechdocs.helpsystems.com/manuals/cobaltstrike/current/userguide/content/topics/appendix-a_unicode-support.htm\"\u003esource\u003c/a\u003e)\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig1-defining-cobalt-strike_hjdr.max-1900x1900.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig1-defining-cobalt-strike_hjdr.max-1100x1100.png 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig1-defining-cobalt-strike_hjdr.max-1900x1900.png 1876w"," 1060px,  1876px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig1-defining-cobalt-strike_hjdr.max-1900x1900.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eWithin the client, operators can import \u003cstrong\u003eAggressor Scripts\u003c/strong\u003e to customize their commands, menu options, and interface. Aggressor Scripts vary in complexity, from adding a new menu shortcut to chaining multiple attack steps. The following is an excerpt from \u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/ramen0x3f/AggressorScripts/blob/master/credpocalypse.cna\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003ecredpocalypse.cna\u003c/a\u003e, an Aggressor Script that checks active beacon sessions on a schedule and runs Mimikatz, an open-source credential dumper, if a new user logs in.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eNote that this is not adding functionality to Cobalt Strike. In this case, the script uses built-in BEACON functionality to list processes and execute Mimikatz, and it uses Cobalt Strike APIs to run on a schedule. That means existing detections for BEACON’s Mimikatz module will also detect this.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,""],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/code1-defining-cobalt-strike_gpzs.max-1000x1000.png",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/code1-defining-cobalt-strike_gpzs.max-1000x1000.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBeacon Object Files \u003c/strong\u003eare single file C programs that are run within a BEACON session. BOFs are expected to be small and run for a short time. Since BEACON sessions are single threaded, BOFs will block any other BEACON commands while they are executing. The following is an example from the \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.cobaltstrike.com/help-beacon-object-files\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eCobalt Strike documentation\u003c/a\u003e that uses Dynamic Function Resolution to look up the current domain.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,""],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/code2-defining-cobalt-strike_eqww.max-1000x1000.png",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/code2-defining-cobalt-strike_eqww.max-1000x1000.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eFinally, \u003cstrong\u003eMalleable Profiles\u003c/strong\u003e allow operators to customize a wide range of settings when they first launch their team server. The snippet that follows from a \u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/rsmudge/Malleable-C2-Profiles/blob/master/normal/amazon.profile\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003epublic profile\u003c/a\u003e is an example of how an operator could make BEACON traffic look like it\u0026#39;s related to Amazon. The portions in blue (the set uri line and the client block), define how a BEACON payload behaves. Some of these values can be extracted from a BEACON sample. See “Interpreting Artifacts \u0026gt; GET and POST Requests” for more information.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,""],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/code3-defining-cobalt-strike_dpbu.max-1000x1000.png",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/code3-defining-cobalt-strike_dpbu.max-1000x1000.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003ch2\u003eImportant Concepts\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eStagers\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eEarlier, I mentioned there are \"two types of BEACON\", one of them being a stager. Operators can have stagers for multiple listener types (e.g. a DNS stager, an SMB stager, an HTTPS stager). In those cases, when the stager shellcode is executed, it will pull the final BEACON payload over the relevant protocol and execute it, establishing a connection using the defined listener method.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAn important note for defenders is that, \u003cem\u003eby default,\u003c/em\u003e defenders can download a Cobalt Strike HTTP/S stager payload from a team server even if the operator is not using staged payloads in their operations. This will allow defenders to 1. confirm something is hosting a team server with a listener on that port and 2. extract additional configuration artifacts from the payload.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis works because Cobalt Strike was designed to be compatible with Metasploit's Meterpreter payload. Metasploit (and thus Cobalt Strike) will serve an HTTPS stager when a valid URL request is received. A valid URL is any 4-character alphanumeric value with a valid 8 bit \u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/rapid7/metasploit-framework/blob/master/lib/rex/payloads/meterpreter/uri_checksum.rb#L6\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003echecksum\u003c/a\u003e calculated by \u003ca href\u003d\"https://isc.sans.edu/forums/diary/Finding+Metasploit+Cobalt+Strike+URLs/27204/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eadding the ASCII values \u003c/a\u003eof the 4 characters.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOperators can prevent defenders from retrieving stagers by setting the host_stage Malleable Profile value to \"false\". More commonly, they may use reverse proxies to filter out unwanted traffic like stager requests. As a protection feature, Cobalt Strike will ignore web requests with blacklisted User-Agents, such as curl or wget. \u003ca href\u003d\"https://blog.zsec.uk/cobalt-strike-profiles/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eStarting in Cobalt Strike 4.4\u003c/a\u003e, operators can also \u003cem\u003ewhitelist\u003c/em\u003e user agents with the .http-config.allow_useragents Malleable Profile option. These caveats are important to remember, since a team server may not always function as expected by scanners that automate stager requests.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAs an operational security note, operators can also detect any web request to a team server, as it will be visible to the operator in their logs\u003cem\u003e.\u003c/em\u003e They will also be able to see in the \"Web Log\" view if a stager has been pulled, along with all HTTP request details like source IP.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eTrial vs Licensed vs Cracked\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eCobalt Strike is not \u003cem\u003elegitimately\u003c/em\u003e freely available. Copies of the team server/client cannot be downloaded as a trial or licensed copy from Help Systems—the company that owns Cobalt Strike—unless the operator applies and has been approved. Unfortunately, trials and cracked copies (including most, if not all, licensed features) have been and continue to be leaked and distributed publicly for nearly all recent versions.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eTrial\u003c/strong\u003e versions of Cobalt Strike are heavily signatured and include lots of obvious defaults intended to be caught in a production environment. (For example, it embeds the EICAR string in all payloads.) This is to ensure that the operator is really using it as a trial and will eventually pay if using it for professional purposes.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eLicensed \u003c/strong\u003eversions of Cobalt Strike include more features (e.g. Arsenal Kits) and fewer embedded artifacts (no more EICAR!). A watermark related to the associated Cobalt Strike license is still embedded in payloads and can be extracted using most BEACON configuration parsers. There are a lot of caveats with that value, so please see \u0026quot;Interpreting Artifacts \u0026gt; Watermarks\u0026quot; later in the post.\r\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eLicenses can be stolen, however if a license is revoked operators will no longer be able to use it to update an installation. If operators keep the \u0026quot;authorization file\u0026quot; (See \u0026quot;Interpreting Artifacts \u0026gt; Watermarks\u0026quot;), the existing installation will still work until expiration.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eCracked\u003c/strong\u003e versions of Cobalt Strike are distributed in various forums. Typically, these are the result of someone modifying a trial JAR file to bypass the license check and rebuilding the JAR, or by crafting an authorization file with a fake license ID and distributing that with the JAR.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eDifferentiating cracked versions of Cobalt Strike from legitimately licensed versions can be difficult. If the watermark was statically defined as part of the cracking process, it may be possible to tie that to a shared/distributed copy. Some cracked copies of Cobalt Strike also contain backdoors to provide third-party access to a team server. In these cases, malware analysts may be able to identify illegitimate copies through static analysis.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eRedirectors\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eInstead of having beacons connect directly to a team server, operators will sometimes use a redirector (or several) that accepts connections and forwards them to the team server. This has several advantages for operators, including being able to:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eCycle through multiple domains for a single BEACON connection\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eReplace detected/blocked redirectors without having to replace the underlying team server\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eUse high(er) reputation domains that help BEACON traffic blend in and avoid detection\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOperators can also use redirectors to filter out \"suspicious\" traffic, like scanners or hunting tools, to protect their team server, however there are typically still easy wins to track down team servers and redirectors. See the \"Hunting for Team Servers\" section for more details.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eWhat About Domain Fronting?\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSometimes \"redirectors\" are as simple as a cloud instance with an nginx proxy, but another highly effective redirector method is \"domain fronting\". Domain fronting is a technique by which an operator may hide the true destination of a network connection by redirecting it through the infrastructure of a Content Delivery Network (CDN). The technique was first documented as a means of bypassing internet censorship and has also been used by threat actors, such as \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.fireeye.com/blog/threat-research/2017/03/apt29_domain_frontin.html\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eAPT 29\u003c/a\u003e, to disguise C2 traffic.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhen an HTTPS request is fronted, the connection is established directly with a reputable domain hosted by the CDN. This is the “fronted” domain. The encrypted request will contain a unique identifier, often contained in the HTTP “Host” header, that the CDN uses to route the request to an operator-controlled server. Because domain fronted traffic is initially sent to the CDN, it will use the legitimate SSL/TLS certificate of the CDN when observed by a defender.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 2: Domain fronted C2 connection (\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.cobaltstrike.com/resources?_sft_cta_type\u003dblog\"\u003esource\u003c/a\u003e)\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig2-defining-cobalt-strike_0_pikz.max-1400x1400.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig2-defining-cobalt-strike_0_pikz.max-1100x1100.png 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig2-defining-cobalt-strike_0_pikz.max-1400x1400.png 1350w"," 1060px,  1350px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig2-defining-cobalt-strike_0_pikz.max-1400x1400.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eIn this situation, to block traffic to the operator’s domain, a defender would need to decrypt the traffic to discover the true destination within an HTTPS connection, but this is not always practical. In addition to being resource intensive, decrypting the traffic for some CDNs may not be possible. For example, some CDNs enforce \u003ca href\u003d\"https://owasp.org/www-community/controls/Certificate_and_Public_Key_Pinning\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003ecertificate pinning\u003c/a\u003e on their SSL/TLS certificates, to prevent interception and decryption of the traffic using an organization-provided trusted root certificate.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eDomain Fronting vs Masquerading\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMasqueraded traffic\u003c/strong\u003e is designed to look like a legitimate service but is actually a \u003cstrong\u003edirect connection\u003c/strong\u003e to an operator’s server, while \u003cstrong\u003efronted traffic\u003c/strong\u003e is \u003cstrong\u003esent to a legitimate service\u003c/strong\u003e (CDN) and forwarded from there to the operator.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIf the Host header value and destination domain are the same, this is just a normal, \u003cem\u003edirect HTTP connection.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIf the Host header and destination domain are different and the Host header is\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eA legitimate domain (e.g. in the Alexa Top 1M), then it is likely \u003cem\u003edomain masquerading\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eA domain used for CDN endpoints (e.g. *.azureedge.net), then it may be \u003cem\u003edomain fronting.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThere are multiple public guides to test if a domain is frontable. \u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/vysecurity/DomainFrontingLists\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eThis repo\u003c/a\u003e also includes a pre-compiled list of frontable domains by CDN provider. Please note, public lists may be out of date and manual validation will likely still be necessary.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eChaining Beacons\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAnother way operators can establish connections is by chaining beacons. Once an operator has a single compromised system, for example beaconing back to an HTTPS listener, they can pivot internally to other systems.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe most common reason an operator will chain beacons is to \u003cstrong\u003ebypass network segmentation\u003c/strong\u003e and restrictions. If they target a server that doesn't have outbound internet access, they can proxy their connection through another beacon with network connectivity to the targeted system and receive the callback on their team server. If the operator loses access to the parent system, they will also lose access to chained beacons.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSMB listeners and SMB staged beacons were the original method for chaining. Cobalt Strike now also supports a raw TCP listener. In environments where the SMB stager is detected (increasingly common for Endpoint Detection and Response products), an operator can use a raw TCP chain over port 445. This will allow them to still leverage the fact that SMB is rarely blocked between internal hosts but avoid using the more heavily signatured SMB listener.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn the screenshot from the \"An Operator's View\" section, the first session’s external IP matches the internal IP for session 2. This indicates it is being chained through the second session. A double infinity/chain icon next to the IP also shows that it is chained. If the system loses connection to its parent host, that chain icon will be disconnected.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe following screenshot shows the “Pivot Graph” view from a Cobalt Strike client. In this graphic, each BEACON is represented by a computer icon. Chained sessions are identified by arrows to their child sessions, and the firewall icon represents an external connection to the operator’s C2.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 3: Client \"graph view\" of chained BEACONs (\u003ca href\u003d\"https://hstechdocs.helpsystems.com/manuals/cobaltstrike/current/userguide/content/topics/listener-infrastructure_beacon-smb.htm?cshid\u003d1040\"\u003esource\u003c/a\u003e)\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig3-defining-cobalt-strike_0_ugoy.max-1400x1400.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig3-defining-cobalt-strike_0_ugoy.max-1100x1100.png 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig3-defining-cobalt-strike_0_ugoy.max-1400x1400.png 1373w"," 1060px,  1373px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig3-defining-cobalt-strike_0_ugoy.max-1400x1400.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eIf responding to an intrusion with SMB or TCP staged BEACON payloads, look for other compromised hosts starting with the IP addresses configured as the “C2” for the staged payloads.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eHunting for Team Servers\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant’s Advanced Practices team regularly scans the internet for C2s, including Cobalt Strike team servers. If an operator does not properly protect their team server, analysts can identify malicious infrastructure without prior knowledge of if or where it is being used and provide advanced warning and coverage for customers. For more details on how we identify C2s like Cobalt Strike, check out our blog post, “\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.mandiant.com/resources/scandalous-external-detection-using-network-scan-data-and-automation\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eSCANdalous! (External Detection Using Network Scan Data and Automation\u003c/a\u003e\u003cu\u003e)\u003c/u\u003e.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eInterpreting Artifacts\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eTeam Server and Client Logs\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eCobalt Strike logs are invaluable for understanding the activities conducted using a team server. Defenders may not frequently have access to this log data, but in the event that logs are available, it is useful to understand what data can be extracted from them.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eCobalt Strike stores logs in two primary formats: \u003cstrong\u003efull, plaintext beacon logs\u003c/strong\u003e and \u003cstrong\u003eJava serialized bins\u003c/strong\u003e. These are stored in the team server working directory and duplicated in client working directories when a client is connected to a team server. They are also updated when items change, such as new credentials being captured.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe \u003cstrong\u003ecobaltstrike/logs/\u003c/strong\u003e directory includes a directory structure of the format \u003cstrong\u003e[date]/[internal ip of beaconed host]/[beacon id].log\u003c/strong\u003e. Each file is a plaintext log of every beacon command and the associated output. This mirrors what an operator would see in the Cobalt Strike beacon console.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe \u003cstrong\u003ecobaltstrike/screenshots/\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e \u003c/strong\u003eand \u003cstrong\u003ecobaltstrike/downloads/\u003c/strong\u003e folders each respectively contain all screenshots or files an operator has downloaded from beacons.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAn individual operator/\u003cu\u003eclient working directory \u003c/u\u003ewill only contain downloads which that operator synchronized to their local system, and these could be from multiple team server connections.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eA \u003cu\u003eteam server working directory\u003c/u\u003e, will contain screenshots/downloads from any operator on that team server. \u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eUnder each \u003cstrong\u003ecobaltstrike/logs/[date]\u003c/strong\u003e directory, there is also an \u003cstrong\u003eevents.log\u003c/strong\u003e and a \u003cstrong\u003eweb.log\u003c/strong\u003e file.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eEvents.log is a mirror of the \"events\" console for the team server which, at a minimum, will include the date/time of each operator connecting to the server and each initial beacon callback.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eWeb.log will show a log of every web request to the team server, including requests for staged payloads or team server-hosted files.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFiles hosted on a team server and served through the Web feature of Cobalt Strike are saved in the \u003cstrong\u003ecobaltstrike/uploads/\u003c/strong\u003e directory. An individual operator/\u003cu\u003eclient working directory\u003c/u\u003e, will only contain files which that operator uploaded.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe \u003cstrong\u003ecobaltstrike/data\u003c/strong\u003e directory includes several .bin files which are serialized Java objects of different data models used by Cobalt Strike to track its state. The data from Cobalt Strike serialized .bin logs can be extracted as CSVs using \u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/ramen0x3f/AggressorScripts/blob/master/parse_cs_bin.py\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003ethis script\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFor most analysts, sessions.bin, listeners.bin, and credentials.bin will be of the highest interest. The .bin logs contain the most concentrated and simple to parse data for identifying which systems and users were compromised using a given team server.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eSessions.bin lists all \u003cu\u003eactive and historical\u003c/u\u003e beacon sessions from the given team server along with some basic metadata (e.g. user, internal and external IP, hostname, architecture). \u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eListeners.bin details all \u003cu\u003eactive\u003c/u\u003e listeners for the given team server.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eCredentials.bin includes any credentials (cleartext, hashed, etc.) that were either automatically dumped with a built-in credential module (such as Mimikatz) or manually added (such as after cracking a password offline). \u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eWatermarks\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eCobalt Strike \u003cstrong\u003ewatermarks\u003c/strong\u003e are a unique value generated from and tied to a given \"CobaltStrike.auth\" file. This value is embedded as the last 4 bytes for all BEACON stagers and in the embedded configuration for \u003ca href\u003d\"https://blog.cobaltstrike.com/2017/09/20/cobalt-strike-3-9-livin-in-a-stagers-paradise/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003efull backdoor BEACON samples\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe \u003cstrong\u003eCobaltStrike.auth\u003c/strong\u003e file is a config file used by Cobalt Strike to determine license ID and expiration. When launched, Cobalt Strike will check that the license is valid and unexpired. The CobaltStrike.auth file is required to launch modern versions of Cobalt Strike, and it is updated when updating Cobalt Strike and when entering a license (whether for the first time or as a re-entry).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe \u003cstrong\u003eCobaltStrike.auth\u003c/strong\u003e file is a config file used by Cobalt Strike to determine license ID and expiration. When launched, Cobalt Strike will check that the license is valid and unexpired. The CobaltStrike.auth file is required to launch modern versions of Cobalt Strike, and it is updated when updating Cobalt Strike and when entering a license (whether for the first time or as a re-entry).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eA \u003cstrong\u003ematching watermark means\u003c/strong\u003e that two payloads came from a team server(s) using the same CobaltStrike.auth file. This \u003cstrong\u003edoes not necessarily mean\u003c/strong\u003e it came from the same operator. Someone can copy the whole Cobalt Strike directory, including the auth file, and install it on another server which would then have the same watermark until the license expired.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003ePublic Keys\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eCobalt Strike uses 4 different types of keystores:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eListeners\u003c/strong\u003e can have custom keystores specified in Malleable Profiles.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eCode signing\u003c/strong\u003e can be configured with custom keystores specified in Malleable Profiles.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eClient Connections\u003c/strong\u003e use the cobaltstrike.store file to encrypt client communications.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBeacon Connections \u003c/strong\u003euse a team server generated keystore to encrypt BEACON data and handle other security features.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe BEACON keys and other security features are discussed in the Raphael Mudge’s training “\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v\u003d3gBJOJb8Oi0\u0026amp;t\u003d2995s\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eAdvanced Threat Tactics: Infrastructure\u003c/a\u003e”. These keys are only used in the full BEACON backdoor, not stagers. As \u003ca href\u003d\"https://research.nccgroup.com/2020/06/15/striking-back-at-retired-cobalt-strike-a-look-at-a-legacy-vulnerability/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eidentified by NCCGroup\u003c/a\u003e, these keys are stored in a serialized file called “.cobaltstrike.beacon_keys” in the team server working directory.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eA \u003cstrong\u003ematching public key means\u003c/strong\u003e that two payloads came from a team server(s) using the same .cobaltstrike.beacon_keys keystore. This \u003cstrong\u003edoes NOT NECESSARILY mean\u003c/strong\u003e it came from the same team server. Again, someone could copy the whole Cobalt Strike directory, including the keystore, as is sometimes done with distributed or cracked copies.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eC2 IPs and URLs\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBEACON parsers will also typically extract C2 IPs, domains, and URLs from samples. Some of these may be legitimate domains, for example if the operator is using domain fronting or masquerading.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThere are some \u003cstrong\u003egotchas\u003c/strong\u003e to be aware of when attempting to cluster BEACON activity for attribution purposes. For example, the following cases do \u003cstrong\u003enot necessarily \u003c/strong\u003emean you're looking at different team servers.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cem\u003eTwo samples connect to different IPs\u003c/em\u003e - This could be the result of changing redirectors or multiple domains/hosts being defined in a listener. \u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cem\u003eTwo samples refer to different URL paths\u003c/em\u003e - Operators can specify multiple URL paths when defining Malleable Profiles. Each time an operator generates BEACON shellcode, Cobalt Strike will select a random value from this list. If two samples have different URLs, it's possible they are still referring to the same server. \u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eGET and POST Requests\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBEACON configurations may include custom HTTP headers, Cookies, etc. for HTTP GET and POST requests. Those fields are representations of how an operator has configured their team server (using Malleable Profile or default values) to construct and disguise the BEACON network traffic. This is \u003cstrong\u003enot\u003c/strong\u003e a direct 1-to-1 for what will be in a payload. Malleable Profiles accept multiple values for several fields, and only one of them may appear in a given payload. If the GET or POST requests don't match exactly, that is not necessarily an indication that it's from a different server.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eSpawn To\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eEach BEACON payload will be configured with two “spawn to” processes, one for 32-bit tasks and one for 64-bit tasks. These values are what BEACON will spawn as temporary processes for various post-exploitation commands. BEACON launches the process, injects into it (with whatever technique the operator has specified), executes the post-exploitation task, and terminates the process. As an example of what commands this may affect, check out this older guide: \u003ca href\u003d\"https://blog.cobaltstrike.com/2017/06/23/opsec-considerations-for-beacon-commands/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eOpsec Considerations for Beacon Commands\u003c/a\u003e. The default spawn to process is rundll32.exe, but this can be modified in two ways. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMalleable Profile\u003c/strong\u003e allows operators to modify the default spawn_to processes (if using a licensed or cracked version). Changing these values will require restarting the team server and regenerating new BEACON payloads to use the new values. \u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eClient\u003c/strong\u003e allows operators to interactively modify the spawn_to processes once a BEACON session is established. These can be changed as many times as desired and don't require a restart, although it will only affect the current BEACON session. \u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eNamed Pipes\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eNamed pipes are a Windows feature used for interprocess communication (IPC). Cobalt Strike uses named pipes in several ways:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePayloads\u003c/strong\u003e - Used to load the backdoor into memory and are modifiable via Malleable profile and/or Artifact Kit. \u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePost-exploitation Jobs\u003c/strong\u003e - Used for a variety of Cobalt Strike commands that need to spawn and inject into a process. \u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eStaging \u003c/strong\u003e- Used for SSH/SMB BEACON staging and chaining\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eRaphael Mudge's post, “\u003ca href\u003d\"https://blog.cobaltstrike.com/2021/02/09/learn-pipe-fitting-for-all-of-your-offense-projects/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eLearn Pipe Fitting for all of your Offense Projects\u003c/a\u003e,“ has some helpful additional details on this feature. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eSleep Time\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe \"sleeptime\" value in a BEACON configuration is the base time used for callback intervals. BEACON will randomize callbacks within a range determined by the \"jitter\" percentage. For example, a sleeptime of 10 seconds and a jitter of 50%, will produce callbacks every 5 to 15 seconds. This value is not constant to make network detection more challenging.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn Malleable Profile, operators can specify a sleeptime in \u003cstrong\u003emilliseconds\u003c/strong\u003e. After a session is created, operators can interactively change the sleeptime and jitter values, but both must be integers and sleeptime must be defined in \u003cstrong\u003eseconds\u003c/strong\u003e. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eKill Date\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe “killdate” value in a BEACON sample dictates whether it should connect to a team server after a given date. This is used by red team operators to ensure payloads are not accidentally executed after an engagement is complete. It can also be used by threat actors to limit successful sandbox execution and frustrate retroactive analysis efforts.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis value is defined by a command line argument when the team server is started, and it is not modifiable through Malleable Profile or interactive BEACON commands. By default, no killdate is specified.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eConclusion\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eCobalt Strike continues to be the C2 framework of choice for both legitimate security testers and threat actors alike. Our hope is that with a more thorough understanding of the framework’s capabilities and common usage, defenders will be more equipped to find new ways to hunt, respond to, and attribute malicious actors using this tool.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eReferences\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eDetection Guides\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca Detection\u003c/a\u003e (Recorded Future)\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/detecting-cobalt-strike-with-memory-signatures\" rel\u003d\"noopener\"
